URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS811865105	2019-10-09 16:06:48	34500	spuh	passerine sp.	Passeriformes sp.			3				United States	US	Nevada	US-NV	Washoe	US-NV-031		9			Thomas Creek Canyon--Trailhead area	L6466160	H	39.3926601	-119.8387197	2019-10-01	18:40:00	obsr98119	S60275385	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	26	0.500		3	1	G4551664	0	1	0				
URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS826864908	2019-11-11 12:46:51	34500	spuh	passerine sp.	Passeriformes sp.			1				United States	US	Nevada	US-NV	Washoe	US-NV-031		9			backyard	L10141199	P	39.5629725	-119.7116194	2019-11-11	08:45:00	obsr1481866	S61365362	Incidental	P20	EBIRD				1	0		0	1	0			Possibly a wood thrush!!!!!----Sitting in my second story window, at 8:45am in Sparks, Nevada (elevation 4500', temperature mid 40s, sunny and calm, quite suburban neighborhood, rivers and streams nearby, despite being in a high desert ecosystem) this bird landed for about 10 seconds in the small outer branches of the naked young elm tree outside my window - maybe eight feet from where I was sitting, so I got a good look for those 10 seconds. We have Northern Flickers bombarding our eves right now and this bird came between flicker "attacks" and sat for a bit. It was slightly smaller than a robin and with a tail shorter than it's body and narrow, like a thrush. The bill was dark along with a dark colored eye and thinner than a robin's but thicker than a wren's and about as long as it's head (again, like a thrush). It sat normally in the tree on very skinny branches, not pointing up like a woodpecker or down like a nuthatch. The tail stuck out from the body at about the same angle as it's back and it was sitting not upright but not completely horizontally oriented - maybe a 20-30 degree angle from horizontal (give or take). It had very uniformly brown - almost reddish brown - coloring across its wings and back. ----What made it stand out to me were the very prominent, intense black and white spots on its underparts, including under it's wings. They were almost as intense as the flicker or a downy woodpecker, but this was definitely not either of those (tail too short, other markings not present, beak wrong, stance in tree wrong), nor was it a juvenile robin (the spots and markings were very clean and neat, like an adult bird). Looking through pictures of possible birds and rare birds for this area and the whole US, the closest I can come to what it looked like is a wood thrush, though even that seems not quite the intensity of black and white spots that I saw, though the brown-red coloration and overall shape, size, and activity of the bird seem to fit. My contacts at the Tahoe Institute for Natural Science are also unsure as it was there and gone so fast I didn't get a chance to take a picture but did take a lot of notes. ----This bird was significantly more spotted - almost checkered - than the Swainson's thrush, and the spots were very dark, not a reddish hue. The spots were also much more intense than a hermit thrush, extending farther down the belly and remaining bright in contrast and crisp around the edges throughout. I'm NOT confident that this was a wood thrush not only because of its location but also because it's intense black and white spots/checkering were more so than an average wood thrush (especially if one was blown so far off course!). However, it is by far the closest bird species I can find to what I saw, and so is the most likely identification of this bird. ----I did not hear it make any noises or get to see it eat anything. I only saw it for those few moments outside my second story window in the bare elm tree in my suburban backyard. I wish I could provide photos as I'm dying to know if it could be a wood thrush or whatever it might be!

The nearest song I can think of to what I heard is a Yellow Breasted Chat. Despite close to a minute of the song, considering my lack of experience in hearing this species in the field, I can't list it as a species sighting. ----The song had a large variety of short phrases, but the phrases were not repeated the way a Northern Mockingbird repeats, and it did not have the same tonal quality. After perhaps a minute, the singing paused and then resumed briefly from a location farther east (downstream). First we walked downstream on the north side of the Truckee river, but didn't hear it again. After that we walked around over the bridge behind the Patagonia factory and east along the service road for the ditch, traversing the whole distance from the bridge nearly to Hunter Creek, but without success in relocating the singer. I am aware that the Yellow Breasted Chat has been sighted along this area in the past. But I don't have the confidence to suggest this as a sighting, in spite of this suspicion.
